 Pressure Altimeter              4
   18.2.1. Terrain Following Radar. A fully functional terrain following system is required for
   TF operations. This includes a source of pitch (INU or vertical gyro [VG]), a mission
   computer, a source of drift (INU or Doppler), and a source of angle of attack (AOA) (mission
   computer).
        18.2.1.1. The AN/APQ-122(V)8 precision ground mapping subsystem with a
        presentation available for the right navigator is required for TF operations in IMC. TF
        operations may be flown in VMC using the procedures outlined in 18.3.2.4.2
   18.2.2. Radar Altimeter. The TF subsystem of the AN/APQ-122(V)8 radar is the primary
   height reference during TF operations. Radar altimeters provide the secondary height
   reference. At least one radar altimeter and both copilot and navigator repeaters must be
   functional for all low-level operations. NOTE: An inoperative low-altitude light on the
   pilot, copilot, or navigator radar altimeter is considered a malfunction. Do not conduct TF or
   NVG operations with an inoperative radar altimeter low-altitude warning light.
   18.2.3. Engine Efficiency. All engines must be operating at a minimum of 98% efficiency
   for TF operations.
18.3. Terrain Following/Terrain Avoidance (TF/TA) Procedures.
   18.3.1. In-flight Crew Duties. The following duties apply while terrain following. The non-
   flying pilot will use NVGs to clear during all night low-level operations.
        18.3.1.1. Pilots:
           18.3.1.1.1. Follow pitch bar commands. WARNING: The AN/APQ-122(V)8 is
           designed for the pitch bar to be used as the primary source of TF pitch commands.
           Do not attempt to fly the E2 "grass" when the use of the pitch bar is denied unless
           positive terrain clearance can be visually verified.
           18.3.1.1.2. The non-flying pilot will monitor clearance in VMC and the pitch bar in
           IMC. Advise the navigators when flying from VMC to IMC and vice versa.
           18.3.1.1.3. Set the radar altimeter altitude clearance set markers at 70% of selected
           radar set clearance. If the radar altimeter low-altitude warning light illuminates,
           climb to a safe altitude.
           18.3.1.1.4. Alternate flying at approximately 30-minute intervals.
           18.3.1.1.5. Accomplish obstacle-warning checks periodically during TF operations.
           WARNING: The pilot will evaluate the E2 display prior to checking the obstacle
           warning (OW) system so as not to mask a valid OW or climb command. NOTE:
           The copilot will alert the crew prior to checking the obstacle warning system. Either
           pilot will alert the crew prior to testing the radar altimeters.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                              137


        18.3.1.1.6. The flying pilot will acknowledge turns by restating the navigator's TA
        heading or the leg course.
        18.3.1.1.7. The flying pilot will ensure turns have been cleared visually or by radar.
        18.3.1.1.8. The flying pilot will receive clearance from the right navigator before
        descending in turns greater than 10 degrees bank angle unless visually cleared by
        pilots. Make only level or climbing turns of no more than 10 degrees bank when the
        right navigator advises weather attenuation prevents him from clearing turns with
        PGM.
     18.3.1.2. Left Navigator:
        18.3.1.2.1. Issues terrain advisories and monitors TA in the direction of turns.
        Immediately direct a roll out, change in bank angle, or climb if terrain will jeopardize
        safety for the established route of flight.
        18.3.1.2.2. Confirms terrain clearance with the right navigator when issuing TA
        headings to the pilot. When clearing the pilot onto navigation system steering, the
        left navigator confirms terrain clearance with the right navigator.
        18.3.1.2.3. Advises pilots of the location and elevation of significant obstructions.
        18.3.1.2.4. Responds to OWs, TF system failures, or weather exceeding system
        limits by restating the MSA after the right navigator has directed any turns away from
        terrain and a climb has been started.
        18.3.1.2.5. Normally performs time control.
        18.3.1.2.6. Advises the pilot and the right navigator when unable to distinguish
        weather from terrain on the left display.
        18.3.1.2.7. May monitor E2 display if pilots are monitoring the TA display.
     18.3.1.3. Right Navigator:
        18.3.1.3.1. Monitors terrain ahead of the airplane using PGM radar and ensures the
        TF radar is providing adequate climb commands, using fan and pencil beams, tilt, and
        filters (STC, FTC, and polarization) as necessary to distinguish terrain from weather
        and to judge the height of terrain above the airplane. WARNING: The mapping
        radar filters (STC, FTC, and polarization) can remove terrain as well as weather from
        the PGM display, if improperly used. NOTE: The map antenna is not stabilized in
        pitch or roll as precisely as the TF antenna, and its tilt control is not calibrated. This
        may cause errors when using pencil beam to judge the relative height of terrain. The
        effect of any error can be nullified by using fan beam to display terrain shadows and
        verify terrain clearance. Disregarding tilt calibration errors, the worst-case height
        error due to vertical stabilization errors is approximately 65 ft for terrain 4 NM from
        the aircraft.
        18.3.1.3.2. Uses PGM radar to ensure all turns, regardless of bank angle, are clear of
        hazardous terrain and adjusts roll out heading or bank angle to maintain clearance.
        The right navigator may clear the pilot to descend in turns provided the PGM radar
        indicator is not attenuated by weather.
138                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


         18.3.1.3.3. Responds to threat maneuvers using PGM to clear turns at bank angles
         requested by the EWO (up to 30 degrees bank) and advises the crew of any possible
         terrain advantage.
         18.3.1.3.4. Advises the pilot and the left navigator when unable to distinguish
         weather from terrain on PGM radar.
         18.3.1.3.5. Uses all available means to update the CANS on a non-interference basis
         with the other required duties.
         18.3.1.3.6. Normally issues TA headings to the pilot flying based on PGM and TA
         radar interpretation and TA plan.
      18.3.1.4. Both navigators monitor aircraft position relative to the planned TA flight path,
      coordinate turn requirements with each other and direct TA turns to the pilot to maintain
      flight path. TA turns should be preplanned with only minor in-flight changes caused by
      terrain not clearly depicted on charts. NOTE: Although the TA flight path is normally
      planned at 10 degrees bank, the right navigator may clear turns up to 30 degrees bank at
      any point to maintain the planned flight path.
      18.3.1.5. EWO:
         18.3.1.5.1. Monitors navigation and maintains awareness of position relative to
         threats and terrain. The EWO will be prepared to supply the pilot with the highest
         altitude allowed, commensurate with the threat environment, for each leg of the
         planned route. The EWO will backup navigator-briefed headings, airspeeds, and
         altitudes.
         18.3.1.5.2. Monitors the electromagnetic environment, providing warning to the pilot
         of possible conflicting traffic with type and clock position.
         18.3.1.5.3. Advises the crew of anticipated threats and probable deviations prior to
         entering the threat area.
         18.3.1.5.4. Supplies maneuver calls, applies appropriate ECM, and informs the crew
         of detected threats with type, position, and mode of engagement.
         18.3.1.5.5. Advises the pilots and navigators when clear of the threat and when able
         to resume planned heading, altitude, and airspeed.
  18.3.2. Specific En Route Procedures. WARNING: Perform an in-flight check of the TF
  system, a level or cresting check, when commencing low-level operations to ensure the
  system is operating within tolerances. If the TF system is flying out of limits low, TF
  operations will not be flown. If the TF system is flying out of limits high, TF operations are
  restricted to VMC.
      18.3.2.1. Changing Selected Altitude Clearance.
         18.3.2.1.1. The pilots and left navigator set the radar altimeter set markers to 70% of
         the new set clearance. The flight engineer verifies the new settings.
         18.3.2.1.2. To descend, the pilot selects the new set clearance, receives permission to
         descend from the right navigator, and descends to the new altitude while monitoring
         the pitch bar. The right navigator monitors the PGM radar throughout the descent.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                                139


Table 18.3. Terrain Following Operations Minimum Altitudes and Visibility.
   Operation          Minimum             Minimum           Remarks/Limitations/Exceptions
                      Altitude            Visibility
 TF or CS Mode        250 ft Set            IMC           During training, conduct IMC low-
                      Clearance                           level under instrument flight rules
                                                          (IFR) unless FLIP or host nation rules
                                                          define other procedures.

Table 18.4. Visual Low-Level Operations Minimum Altitude and Visibility.
   Operation          Minimum            Minimum            Remarks/Limitations/Exceptions
                       Altitude           Visibility
 Visual              300 ft contour      Day VMC 1

                        MSA 2
                                         Night VMC        MSAs may be segmented.
 NVG                 300 ft contour        3 SM 1         Flat to rolling terrain

                     500 ft contour
                                           3 SM 1         Mountainous terrain
 Threat                100 ft AGL          VMC 1          Threat penetrations limited to coastal
 Penetration                                              penetration and flat to rolling terrain
 Notes:
 1. Pilots must be able to visually acquire controlling terrain at least 3 SM from the aircraft
 along the projected route of flight. Visual reference to the ground must be maintained and a
 visible horizon is recommended.
 2. Climbs must be initiated so that the aircraft is established at the higher MSA prior to the
 next segmented point. Descent to a lower MSA will not be initiated until past the next
 segmented point.
          18.3.2.1.3. To climb, the pilot starts the climb and monitors the pitch bar. The pilot
          will select the new set clearance after reaching an altitude that will not activate the
          OW system.
       18.3.2.2. Changing Control of the Aircraft. The pilot flying the aircraft will state the
       desired ground speed and leg course or TA heading. The pilot not flying the aircraft
       repeats the speed and heading and ensures a positive change of control. NOTE: If either
       pilot must leave his seat during TF operations, climb to at least the 500 ft set clearance in
       VMC. Climb to MSA if flying NVG contours or in IMC.
       18.3.2.3. Evasive maneuvers. For specific terminology, refer to AFTTP 33.MC130. A
       hard break is a visual maneuver and the pilots must clear terrain prior to initiation. When
       operating in IMC, the navigators will clear for terrain in accordance with paragraphs
       18.3.1.3 and 18.3.1.4 WARNING: Practicing defensive threat maneuvering does not
       constitute authority to deviate from limitations in the flight manual.
       18.3.2.4. Aircraft Systems Failure. Begin an immediate climb to MSA. After
       troubleshooting, either resume TF/TA operations or transition to alternate low-level
       procedures.
 140                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


          18.3.2.4.1. Engine Out. Discontinue TF operations, if feasible. If terrain following
          is necessary, select the 170K gross weight setting and fly in the highest set clearance
          commensurate with the threat (1,000 ft set clearance for training).
          18.3.2.4.2. Loss of PGM (KA R/T, ECA or map antenna fail).
              18.3.2.4.2.1. The pilot selects CS mode and makes only level or climbing turns of
              up to 10 degrees of bank.
              18.3.2.4.2.2. The right navigator may monitor TA to permit the left navigator to
              perform visual navigation as conditions permit.
              18.3.2.4.2.3. Conditions permitting, the pilots will visually clear all turns using
              NVGs. The bank angle used for visually cleared turns will be at the discretion of
              the pilots.
          18.3.2.4.3. Loss of DSC Channel. When one or more DSC channels fail, the lowest
          priority operators must share a channel.     NOTE: During training missions the
          following two procedures are restricted to VMC only.
          18.3.2.4.4. Pilot‘s DSC Indicator Display Failure. Continue TF operations using the
          pitch bar alone.
          18.3.2.4.5. Loss or Erratic Operation of the Pitch Bar. Continue TF operations using
          the E2 display only.
18.4. NVG Low-level Operations.
   18.4.1. General. Refer to AFTTP 33.MC130 and Chapter 17 of this regulation for detailed
   information on NVG low-level planning factors and limitations.
   18.4.2. Required NVG Equipment.
       18.4.2.1. Aircraft Kits. Aircraft blackout curtains, green fabric tape, and porthole covers.
       18.4.2.2. Aircrew Kit. IR filtered flashlights, chemical lights (covert and overt) and
       NVGs. The pilots will use helmet-mounted NVGs. The left navigator and flight
       engineer may use a hand held set of NVGs. Have a spare set of NVGs available in the
       cockpit at a minimum. Pilots may choose to have their own spare set immediately
       available for them in flight. When the mission requires the loadmasters to use NVGs, a
       spare set will be available for their use as well. All individuals must perform a thorough
       preflight of their NVGs. Pilots are responsible for pre-flighting the spare set of NVGs in
       the cockpit and their own spares if carried.
       18.4.2.3. Pressure Altimeters. Both pilots‘, the navigator's, and the EWO's pressure
       altimeter will be operational.
       18.4.2.4. CANS. At least one navigation subsystem must be operational (INU, GPS).
       18.4.2.5. Training Restrictions. Aircraft flying NVG low-level will have an operating
       PGM (Ka-Band) radar for training flights conducted in mountainous terrain.
   18.4.3. Crew Duties:
       18.4.3.1. Flying Pilot. Maintain contour altitude and ensure terrain clearance.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                             141


       18.4.3.2. Non-flying Pilot. Responsible for backing up the flying pilot on terrain and
       obstacle clearance. Should help the navigator maintain proper flight path, and
       acquiring/identifying con-trolling terrain and the objective area.
       18.4.3.3. Flight Engineer.    Monitors instruments, cross-checks altitude, and terrain
       clearance.
       18.4.3.4. Left Navigator (LN). Gives terrain and climb/descent advisories to the pilots.
       If seated, briefs turn points, manages TOT control and calls warnings. If the PGM is
       denied, the LN will use NVGs to assist in terrain clearance, controlling terrain
       acquisition, and maintaining desired flight path.
       18.4.3.5. Right Navigator (RN). If LN is in the window chart reading, briefs turn points,
       manages TOT control, and calls drop warnings. Uses equipment to keep the CANS
       updated and cross-checks altitude and terrain clearance. Gives TA headings based on
       radar, if use of PGM is not denied.
       18.4.3.6. EWO. Monitors radar warning receiver (RWR) equipment and advises of the
       threat situation. Verifies all recommended altitudes and turn point briefs.
   18.4.4. Transition to NVG Operations. Run the BEFORE LOW-LEVEL checklist if not
   already completed. Before transitioning from TF/TA to NVG low-level operations, ensure
   the aircraft has been properly configured (NVGs on and focused, blackout curtains lowered,
   aircraft lighting set up). If the aircraft has not been properly configured, climb to MSA.
   18.4.5. En route Procedures. NOTE: If either pilot must leave the seat, climb to MSA.
       18.4.5.1. If a threat forces the aircraft to deviate, the crew must make the decision to
       continue NVG low-level procedures or transition to TF operations. The decision should
       take into account terrain, illumination/visual conditions, status of the threat, and radar
       warm-up time. During training, threat engagements will be terminated if the aircraft
       deviates more than 2 NM from the planned flight path or as briefed.
   18.4.6. Transition from NVG Low-Level. Maintain the desired TF altitude if transitioning to
   TF/TA flight. Begin a climb if not transitioning to TF/TA flight and complete the AFTER
   LOW-LEVEL checklist.
18.5. SCA Procedures. This operation requires a mission crew.
   18.5.1. Non-NVG Procedures:
       18.5.1.1. The pilot and navigator will brief and review the SCA during the ENROUTE
       portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist.
       18.5.1.2. Prior to the initial point (IP), the navigators will check the IP-LZ data in the
       CANS. The navigators will use all available means to verify position and terrain or
       obstruction clearance before descending below TF set clearance (MSL altitude if the TF
       radar is not used).
       18.5.1.3. Approaching the IP, initiate the turn to roll out on final. After the turn is
       initiated, the navigators will confirm the CANS is in the approach mode and notify the
       pilots of IP-LZ steering and cross track.
142                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


      18.5.1.4. Normally, the navigator will initiate the slowdown no later than 3 NM prior to
      glide slope intercept. The pilot flying the aircraft will slow to 140 KIAS or 50%
      approach speed, whichever is higher. The pilot flying the aircraft will call for flaps to
      50% and the copilot will lower the gear at the appropriate speeds. The pilot will not
      move the pitch signal select switch to the isolate position until terrain following is no
      longer required.
      18.5.1.5. On final approach, the left navigator will make distance to touchdown and
      cross track left or right of LZ centerline calls using the CANS (backed up by the mapping
      radar). The right navigator will confirm LZ centerline alignment on short final with
      mapping radar. The right navigator will give final verbal headings when the radar
      presentation indicates the aircraft is not aligned with the LZ centerline. The pilot not
      flying the approach will monitor the CANS or a ground approach aid.
      18.5.1.6. The aircraft will be slowed to normal or max effort threshold speed, as
      required. Do not extend flaps beyond 50% until terrain following is no longer required.
      For tactical operations, the pilot will set the desired egress detent and may isolate the
      pitch bar. The engineer completes the BEFORE LANDING checklist.
      18.5.1.7. At glide slope interception, the left navigator will state "Begin Descent" and
      repeat the desired descent rate. The left navigator will provide advisories with reference
      to the CANS, the servoed pressure altimeter, and the radar altimeter. These calls will be
      made at 1 NM intervals until start descent and then will be made at ½ NM intervals until
      reaching 300 ft above the TDZE, or when the pilot takes visual control, whichever occurs
      first. State deviations in excess of 150 ft explicitly.
      18.5.1.8. The left navigator, backed up by the EWO or AMSS, will call "100 Above" at
      MDA plus 100 ft and "Minimums" at MDA. The pilot flying the aircraft will
      acknowledge the MDA call.
      18.5.1.9. The left navigator calls "Missed Approach Point" at zero to go on the CANS.
  18.5.2. NVG SCA Procedures. Use the Non-NVG SCA procedures and add the following:
      18.5.2.1. General. An NVG landing qualified pilot (non-instructor) may takeoff and land
      from the right seat provided an NVG landing qualified pilot is in the left seat and normal
      (non-assault) procedures are used. Either pilot may fly the NVG SCA. Procedures
      described are for the copilot flying the SCA. Use normal performance unless max effort
      performance is required.
      18.5.2.2. Takeoff Procedures:
         18.5.2.2.1. Pilot. At takeoff speed, the pilot rotates to establish a seven-degree nose-
         up attitude. When the aircraft is in a definite climb, verified by the copilot, the pilot
         calls for gear retraction. The pilot may continue to fly or direct the copilot to take
         control. This positive change of control should not occur at less than 100 ft AGL.
             18.5.2.2.1.1. On takeoff roll, the copilot calls out airspeeds starting at 60 KIAS
             and continues to call every 10 knots. These calls are optional.
             18.5.2.2.1.2. After rotation, the copilot checks and verifies a seven-degree nose-
             up attitude and positive rate of climb are maintained. The copilot raises the gear
             on command. The copilot may take control of the aircraft when directed by the
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                           143


           pilot. Flaps should remain set at 50% until reaching a safe airspeed and altitude.
           The pilot flying the aircraft directs the accomplishment of the AFTER TAKEOFF
           checklist.
        18.5.2.2.2. Flight Engineer. Using NVGs, will back up the pilot during takeoff roll
        and climb-out. Clears for terrain and obstacles.
        18.5.2.2.3. Navigator. The navigator will provide a departure briefing including
        departure heading and terrain and obstacles. The navigator will call altitudes at 50,
        100, 150, and 200 ft AGL during climb-out. These calls are optional.
     18.5.2.3. Approach Procedures.
        18.5.2.3.1. Pilot:
           18.5.2.3.1.1. Upon visual acquisition of the runway state "Runway In Sight,
           Pilot" and may direct the copilot to the runway or maneuver the aircraft to runway
           alignment. The pilot will take control of the aircraft and make the landing. The
           copilot may make airspeed and descent calls if requested by the pilot.
           18.5.2.3.1.2. Upon landing and if briefed prior, the pilot may continue to receive
           airspeed calls from the copilot until 60 KIAS or taxi speed.
           18.5.2.3.1.3. Taxi aircraft into position and give the loadmaster clearance to on or
           off-load by stating "Clear To Off-load/Onload."
        18.5.2.3.2. Copilot:
           18.5.2.3.2.1. The copilot normally flies the aircraft from approximately the 20-
           minute warning until the pilot takes control of the aircraft on final approach. The
           copilot may lower the NVGs, if desired, but must not allow distraction from the
           primary duty of flying the approach and, if necessary, executing a go-around.
           18.5.2.3.2.2. The copilot will maintain the planned set clearance or MSL altitude
           until the descent point and follow the CANS steering or navigator's instructions.
           18.5.2.3.2.3. The copilot will not descend below MDA and will maintain the
           MDA until the pilot takes control or until reaching the missed approach point.
           Execute a go-around if the runway environment is not in sight at the MAP,
           directed, or a safe landing cannot be made.
           18.5.2.3.2.4. Once the pilot has taken control, the copilot may provide the pilot
           with air-speed and descent rate information if requested by the pilot. Intersperse
           this information with the HAT calls from the navigator: For example, "115,
           Down 6, 50 ft."
           18.5.2.3.2.5. Upon landing, the copilot will provide airspeed to the pilot until
           slowing to 60 KIAS. These calls are optional. The copilot will have NVGs
           focused and ready for immediate use during taxi operations and assist in locating
           the briefed location.
        18.5.2.3.3. Navigator: Call all warnings for the INFIL/EXFIL checklist (60-, 30-
        advisories, 20-, and 10-minute warnings).
           18.5.2.3.3.1. Intersperse height above touchdown calls with the copilot's airspeed
144                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


             and descent rate calls if briefed. EXCEPTION: The navigator will call 2 NM
             and minimums to alert the loadmaster to initiate the "One Minute Warning" and
             "Prepare To Land" calls respectively.
         18.5.2.3.4. Flight Engineer. The flight engineer's primary attention should be
         directed inside the cockpit. Assist in clearing for other aircraft or obstacles only
         when other duties permit.
             18.5.2.3.4.1. Cross check airspeeds, altitudes and headings. Check external
             lighting and cockpit for bleed through of non-IR light. Have NVGs available and
             ready for use prior to the IP. Assist in maintaining terrain and obstacle clearance.
             18.5.2.3.4.2. Call "Configuration Checked" after checking gear down and the
             flaps during the BEFORE LANDING portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist.
             Assist in field acquisition and call "Runway In Sight, Engineer" once the runway
             is identified. NOTE: An additional pilot may accomplish this step if the flight
             engineer is noncurrent in NVG landings.
             18.5.2.3.4.3. Sets the flaps and exterior lights during the BEFORE LANDING
             portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist.
             18.5.2.3.4.4. Assist in finding the off-load point. Clear for other aircraft and
             obstructions during taxi operations.
      18.5.2.4. Go-around. A go-around will be initiated at the MAP if the runway has not
      been visually acquired, a safe landing cannot be accomplished, or if directed.
         18.5.2.4.1. Pilots. The flying pilot will initiate the go-around and ensure a positive
         rate of climb is obtained and verified by the non-flying pilot. A change of aircraft
         control may be accomplished during the go-around but should not occur lower than
         100 ft AGL. Reconfigure the flaps and gear once a safe altitude and airspeed have
         been reached. Reset the TF radar set clearance and pitch bar (if applicable).
             18.5.2.4.1.1. If a go-around occurs but an immediate landing is feasible, the pilot
             may maneuver to a VFR pattern. The NVG VFR pattern must be part of the
             overall planning. The pilot will announce intentions and direct the engineer to set
             the flaps to 50%. The pilot will announce the planned altitude and airspeed for
             downwind. The flight engineer will complete the asterisked items of the
             BEFORE LANDING portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist. After the base turn
             is initiated all operations will be flown for a NVG landing.
         18.5.2.4.2. Flight Engineer. Backs up the pilots on airspeed, altitude, rate of climb,
         and terrain clearance.
         18.5.2.4.3. Navigators. Repeat the set clearance or MSL altitude and initial heading
         required if a go-around is executed. When a go-around is initiated below 200 ft AGL,
         call altitudes at 50, 100, 150, and 200 ft AGL during the climb. These calls are
         optional.
      18.5.2.5. The FLIR Pass is a method of ensuring an uncontrolled LZ is clear using the
      FLIR. Complete the Ten-minute warning on time and hold the checklist at this point.
      Normally, the copilot will fly during the FLIR pass. As a guide, at a pre-briefed point
      prior, slow the aircraft to 180 KIAS using the verbiage "Slow To 180 Now" (called by
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                             145


      the navigator). Normally the aircraft is flown at 500 ft AGL, aligned 100-200 yards to
      the right of the runway. This allows the pilot and navigator to "visually" acquire any
      obstacles placed on the runway. As soon as possible the navigator will call "Runway
      Clear (or condition)" and begin to retract the FLIR. Turn to a downwind, maintaining the
      planned set clearance or MSL altitude, and resume the INFIL/EXFIL checklist. After the
      base turn, all operations will be flown for a NVG landing.
      18.5.2.6. Aircraft NVG Preparations. Tape all non-NVG compatible lighting and select
      NVIS with the NVIS Lighting Mode Switch. All crew positions should tape all bright
      indicator lights, dim all panel lights, and close blackout curtains as applicable.
18.6. Airdrop Procedures.
   18.6.1. Navigating to the Release Point.
      18.6.1.1. General. Positioning at the release point is the most critical phase of the
      airdrop mission. Ensure all warnings and checklists are completed, proper DZ line-up is
      maintained, and the TOT is within established tolerances.
      18.6.1.2. Positioning. CANS AUTOCARP, manual CARP, manual beacon, and ground
      marked release system (GMRS) methods may be used to position the aircraft at the
      release point. For visual airdrops at night, the left navigator may wear night vision
      goggles (NVG) to accomplish drops on covertly marked or unmarked drop zones. The
      navigators are responsible for computing the release point for all airdrops (EXCEPTION:
      GMRS).
      18.6.1.3. Aircrew Procedures. These are basic procedures used to arrive at the release
      point.
          18.6.1.3.1. The left navigator will brief the crew on CARP location, the expected
          drift, magnetic heading, drop altitude, drop airspeed, MSA between IP and DZ,
          ground hazards and terrain in the drop area, escape heading, and altitude to be flown
          after the drop. The navigator will brief an updated CARP after slowdown if it has
          changed.
          18.6.1.3.2. Airspeed adjustments for TOT should be completed prior to slowdown.
          18.6.1.3.3. The navigator provides course corrections that will position the aircraft
          the required distance upwind from the centerline track so large corrections will not be
          required on the final approach.
          18.6.1.3.4. Upon departing the IP, the right navigator will confirm that IP - DZ
          steering is selected. The left navigator will select manual on the Cargo Release
          Control Panel at the 10-minute warning. This action will preclude inadvertent
          triggering of the system during the IP to DZ run in.
          18.6.1.3.5. The pilot will steer to the CARP using the flight director system or
          following the navigator's headings.
          18.6.1.3.6. If required, the navigator will call the slowdown. A 6 NM slowdown is
          normally used, but may be modified. A modified slowdown can be planned or
          briefed in flight prior to the slowdown. The modified slowdown allows the navigator
          to call the 1-minute warning before the slowdown check is complete. Loadmasters
146                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


         will complete slowdown and 1-minute checks in sequence and then call them
         complete.
         18.6.1.3.7. The navigators will advise when or if terrain following will terminate and
         request the pilot to fly the appropriate MSL altitude and airspeed. This normally
         takes place at the slowdown but may be modified due to terrain or obstacles. When
         MSL altitudes are flown, the pressure altimeter is the primary height reference, with
         the radar altimeter set to 50 ft below drop altitude. When the TF radar is used as the
         primary height reference, set the radar altimeter to 70% of set clearance.
         18.6.1.3.8. After the ten-second call, if it is determined that a safe AUTOCARP can
         be made, the navigator may select auto.
         18.6.1.3.9. If it is determined that a safe AUTOCARP cannot be made and conditions
         permit, a backup visual drop can be made. Ensure the manual release button has
         manual selected in order to preclude inadvertent triggering of the system.
         18.6.1.3.10. Ten seconds prior to the CARP, the navigator will give a verbal
         warning, "Ten Seconds."
         18.6.1.3.11. At the CARP:
             18.6.1.3.11.1. The navigator will give the command "Green Light."
             18.6.1.3.11.2. The non-flying pilot will turn on the green light, state ―On,‖ and
             simultaneously depress the aerial delivery system (ADS) button (if applicable).
             18.6.1.3.11.3. The loadmaster, after seeing the green light and hearing the oral
             signal, will activate the manual release handle if the extraction chute fails to
             release (if applicable).
         18.6.1.3.12. During the Drop:
             18.6.1.3.12.1. The flying pilot will maintain the desired track, making small
             corrections as necessary.
             18.6.1.3.12.2. The navigator will call "Red Light" at the end of the programmed
             or computed time delay, or load clear.
             18.6.1.3.12.3. If the loadmaster observes any delay or malfunction of equipment,
             or delay of a parachutist to jump, the pilot will be advised.
         18.6.1.3.13. Hostile Drop Zone Escape. After the aircraft has been reconfigured (for
         drops requiring a slowdown), the static lines have been retrieved or cut and the
         paratroop doors and deflectors closed, the remainder of the completion of drop
         checklist may be deferred to a time when threat avoidance does not take precedence.
  18.6.2. AUTOCARP. This is the primary method of airdrop. When waypoint and ballistic
  data are programmed, the CANS computes the CARP, provides navigational steering through
  the pilot's flight director system, and activates the green light. The right navigator normally
  updates the CANS with GPS or radar, providing an accurate blind airdrop capability.
  WARNING: AUTOCARP release will not be used for personnel airdrops. NOTE: The
  airdrop accuracy of this method is directly dependent on the accuracy of pre-computed
  relative radar targets and DZ coordinates. For this reason, both navigators will compute and
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                              147


  plot the coordinates or offsets of planned relative radar targets and point of impact
  independently, then resolve any discrepancies prior to programming the CANS.
  18.6.3. Visual Airdrop Procedures. When visual markings or references are available and
  visibility permits, this method allows the left navigator or pilots to make final adjustments in
  aircraft alignment and call the drop visually. The left navigator or pilot directing the drop
  must ensure the flying pilot is notified when to fly headings versus following the CANS
  steering.
     18.6.3.1. The left navigator, with the assistance of the right navigator, determines the
     drop wind then solves and plots the CARP.
     18.6.3.2. The left navigator informs the pilots and the right navigator of the final CARP
     location relative to the point of impact.
     18.6.3.3. As soon as the DZ is visible and identified by the left navigator and pilot, they
     jointly confirm the release point location, lateral offset, and track required. The pilot
     flying the aircraft then assumes the responsibility for maintaining the desired track.
     NOTE: When the left navigator is using NVGs for primary or backup visual airdrops
     and is forward of the blackout curtain, the navigators should coordinate warning calls,
     drop winds, and CARP location over private interphone.
 148                                               AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


                                          Chapter 19

                        MC130H EMPLOYMENT PROCEDURES

19.1. General. This chapter describes the specific equipment requirements and restrictions for
MC130H Combat Talon II employment. It provides the directives for aircraft employment and
operational procedures.
19.2. Low-level Procedures.
   19.2.1. Minimum Operating Equipment. Aircrew experience level and the mission scenario
   may dictate more restrictive equipment requirements than those listed in the following tables.
