          navigator and PM will confirm that the airdrop leg and sensitive steering are active.
          21.8.5.1.2. The navigator will confirm DZ alignment with radar. If on board, the
          CSO will confirm DZ alignment and DZ status using M1/M2.
          21.8.5.1.3. The navigator and PM will visually confirm SCNS position using
          preplanned visual update points and provide corrections if required. WARNING:
          The PF must be aware of paratroopers standing in the back and avoid abrupt pitch or
          bank changes after the Six Minute Advisory.
      21.8.5.2. Slowdown. Slowdown at a distance commensurate with the proficiency of
      crew, atmospheric conditions (pressure altitude and outside air temperature), and the
      threat environment.
          21.8.5.2.1. All controlled ETA procedures should be completed prior to reaching the
          slowdown point.
          21.8.5.2.2. After the navigator calls for the slowdown, the flying pilot will
          acknowledge the slowdown call and reduce the throttles (0-1,000 in-lbs of torque).
      21.8.5.3. Release point.
          21.8.5.3.1. During VMC operations, the PM and the navigator will confirm the
          aircraft is positioned within the pre-briefed VMC safety box.
          21.8.5.3.2. During IMC operations, both pilots and the navigator will confirm the
          aircraft is positioned within the pre-briefed IMC safety box.
      21.8.5.4. Escape. The navigator will call "Red Light" at the expiration of the usable DZ
      time or upon hearing the LM call "Load Clear," whichever occurs first.
          21.8.5.4.1. Begin the escape procedure no earlier than the ―Load Clear‖ call.
          21.8.5.4.2. After the paratroop doors and air deflectors are closed and the flaps have
          been reconfigured, the remainder of the COMPLETION OF DROP checklist may be
          deferred if required for threat avoidance.
   21.8.6. Airdrop malfunctions and Off-DZ airdrops. Reporting of airdrop malfunctions and
   off-DZ airdrops will be AFJI 13210 and AFI 13217. Local reporting procedures will be
   detailed in the group or unit supplement to this instruction.
21.9. Receiver Air-to-Air Refueling.
   21.9.1. Conduct IAW T.O. 1C130(M)W1 and ATP56(B).
   21.9.2. LAIRCM may be left in OPERATE mode during AAR.
21.10. Forward Area Refueling Point.
   21.10.1. The MC-130W will conduct single-point and two-point FARP operations without a
   forward area manifold (FAM) cart. Three-point FARP operations using a FAM cart will not
   normally be conducted.
 176                                                  AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


   21.10.2. FARP planning and execution will be conducted IAW AFI 11235.
21.11. Armed Overwatch.
   21.11.1. Armed overwatch will be conducted using the PRECISION STRIKE MISSION
   checklist. During armed overwatch, the navigator repositions to the cargo compartment to
   perform primary crew duties on the MOP.
   21.11.2. OB Analysis. The CSO and navigator will coordinate with intelligence personnel to
   obtain a current and complete OB for the area of operations. Based on analysis of the OB,
   the CSO and navigator will ensure the planned route of flight minimizes aircraft exposure to
   the threat and the probability of detection.
   21.11.3. Chart Preparation. Use 1:50,000 (TLM) for all armed overwatch objective areas.
   Grid lines must be clearly displayed for accurate point annotation. A 10 NM MSA will be
   computed for every assigned objective area.
   21.11.4. Ground Operations.
       21.11.4.1. Landings (including touch and go) at jointuse or military fields with 30MM
       ammunition or SOPGM on board requires a letter of agreement on file between OGV, or
       COMAFSOF, and the airfield manager. Publish a list of approved fields in Chapter 10.
          21.11.4.1.1. If an emergency situation requires a full stop at the airfield, upon
          termination of the emergency, the PIC will attempt to ensure the aircraft is parked
          IAW Table 21.3 or as defined in the letter of agreement.
       21.11.4.2. Parking with Munitions. If required to park with munitions on board, comply
       with the separation distances given in Table 21.3 These separation distances are the
       minimum required between explosives loaded aircraft and any inhabited building,
       civilian aircraft, or civilian/joint use runway. All explosives-loaded aircraft should be
       parked in an approved, properly sited aircraft parking location if available.

Table 21.3. Parking Separation Distances.
    Munitions Type            Hazard             Unrelated         Related          Aircraft-to-
                           Class/Division        Facilities1      Facilities2        Aircraft3
 SOPGM and 30MM                 1.1D              1,250 ft          143 ft
 SOPGM Only                     1.1D               789 ft           100 ft              238 ft
 30MM Only                     1.2.2E              168 ft           100 ft
 Notes:
 1. Any inhabited building, civilian aircraft, or civilian/joint-use runway not associated with
 MC-130W operations.
 2. Includes maintenance facilities/structures; may be superseded by site survey results.
 3. Distance is measured from cargo bay to cargo bay when munitions are internal. IAW
 AFMAN 91-201, reduced separation requires written risk acceptance by the commander
 responsible for the aircraft.
       21.11.4.3. Emergency Procedures with Ammunition. Fire:
          21.11.4.3.1. Notify PIC/crew.
          21.11.4.3.2. Notify fire department.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                            177


         21.11.4.3.3. Fight fire and remove munitions, if possible. Note time fire envelops
         munitions.
         21.11.4.3.4. All non-essential personnel will withdraw to 2,500 ft. Crew members
         fighting the fire will withdraw to 2,500 ft when fire envelops munitions, or after
         arrival of firefighters, whichever occurs first. If the Net Explosive Weight (NEW)
         exceeds 500 pounds with SOPGM on board, non-essential personnel must withdraw
         to 4,000 ft.
         21.11.4.3.5. With only division 1.3 munitions (markers and flares) on board, non-
         essential personnel must withdraw to 600 ft.        NOTE: Withdraw distance
         requirements are contained in AFMAN 91201.
  21.11.5. Crew Duties. General crew member responsibilities during armed overwatch:
     21.11.5.1. The PIC is primary on flying the aircraft and is the final authority on
     weapons/fuzing selection and weapons release parameters.
     21.11.5.2. The copilot is primary for SCNS operation and ATC, C2, and air stack
     communications.
     21.11.5.3. While stationed on the MOP, the navigator is primary for M1 sensor
     operations, ground party communications, weaponeering, and targeting.
     21.11.5.4. The CSO is primary for M2 sensor operations, ground party communications,
     CAS/on-station check-ins, battlefield SA and friendly position SA.
     21.11.5.5. Once the navigator transitions to the MOP, the FE assumes control of all flight
     deck navigator panel equipment and is primary for monitoring defensive systems.
     21.11.5.6. The LMs are primary for scanner duties and gun/SOPGM monitoring and
     operation, to include movement of SOPGMs between the storage rack and the cargo
     door.
  21.11.6. Prestrike Coordination.
     21.11.6.1. Accomplish the COMBAT ENTRY checklist prior to initiating the
     PRESTRIKE checklist. Mission timeline permitting, complete the PRESTRIKE
     checklist prior to reaching the objective area (restricted operating zone (ROZ), strike
     environment, etc.).
     21.11.6.2. During the PRESTRIKE checklist the navigator will give a navigation hand
     off briefing to the pilots and flight engineer. The briefing will cover: radar set up, SCNS
     settings, bullseye, steering, defensive system settings, MEA, MSA, and general situation
     (to include distances to ROZs or other SUA).
     21.11.6.3. Navigation. Once the navigator has transitioned to the MOP, the pilots and
     flight engineer are responsible for en route and tactical navigation. SCNS will be
     primary for en route navigation. Tactical navigation can be performed via Brimstone or
     SCNS.
         21.11.6.3.1. Brimstone will normally be the primary means of navigation within the
         tactical environment. The CSO will ensure the Brimstone airspace overlays are
         loaded in the system on the auxiliary computer. The copilot will pull the draw file up
 178                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


           on the copilot side monitor and, unless briefed otherwise, the pilot side monitor.
           Workload permitting, the copilot/FE should enter relevant tactical navigation points
           into SCNS throughout the mission. In the event of Brimstone degradation, the copilot
           or FE will input tactical navigation points into SCNS.
       21.11.6.4. Laser usage. Nominal ocular hazard distances (NOHD) are given in Table
       21.4

Table 21.4. Nominal Ocular Hazard Distances.
        Sensor             Laser Designator        Laser Range Finder        Laser Illuminator
 MX-15i                            n/a                   Eye-safe                   365 m
 MX-15Di                          19 km                    19 km                    365 m
          1
 (w/CLDR )
 MX-20HADD                27 km (1.06 micron) 27 km (1.06 micron)                   735 m
          2
 (w/SWLS )                                          80 m (1.57 micron)
 Notes:
 1. Combined laser designator/range finder (CLDR).
 2. Switchable wavelength laser subsystem (SWLS).
 WARNING: The Navigator/CSO will notify the crew prior to firing the MX-20HADD 1.06
 micron laser or MX-15Di CLDR. On training missions they will only be fired on laser-
 approved live-fire ranges. If ground parties are present they will be advised prior to lasing.

 WARNING: Loadmasters will not use binoculars during any lasing operations when weather
 is in close proximity to the aircraft or anytime the M2 is firing a non-eye-safe laser.

 NOTE: Aircrews will use J-Laser terminology to the maximum extent practical.

   21.11.7. Live-fire Operations.
       21.11.7.1. Maintain a minimum of 1,000 ft altitude separation from the highest known
       trajectory during combined live-fire operations with ground artillery, mortar, or
       helicopter fire.
       21.11.7.2. Danger Close distances are given in Table 21.5

Table 21.5. Danger Close Distances.
                  Munition                                  Danger Close Distance
 Griffin                                                           140 m
 ViperStrike                                                         61 m
 30MM                                                                 TBD

       21.11.7.3. Peacetime Restrictions. The following restrictions apply to all peacetime live-
       fire training missions. These restrictions do not apply anytime the GFC requests fire
       support under CJCSI 3121.10, Standing Rules of Engagement for U.S. Forces.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                             179


           21.11.7.3.1. Minimum safe distances and ricochet fans are given in Table 21.6
           During training, fire no closer to ground parties than the minimum safe distance. If
           the ground party is located within the ricochet fan distance, use a no-fire zone to
           mitigate ricochet risk. The no-fire zone can be defined by radials from the target or
           (30MM only) by aircraft heading.
               21.11.7.3.1.1. No-fire radials are +/- 30 degrees from the friendly position to
               target bearing.
               21.11.7.3.1.2. To compute aircraft no-fire headings, determine the bearing from
               the friendly position to the target. Subtract 60 degrees to get the heading
               corresponding to the start of the no-fire zone. Subtract another 60 degrees to get
               the heading corresponding to the end of no-fire zone.

Table 21.6. Peacetime Training Restrictions.
           Munition                 Minimum Safe Distance                  Ricochet Fan
 Griffin                                   N/A1                                N/A
 ViperStrike                                  N/A1                              N/A
 30MM                                         TBD                               TBD
 Note 1: Hazard areas for guided weapons are highly dependent upon launch conditions and
 coordinate accuracies.
 WARNING: During peacetime, do not point a loaded and armed weapon at a friendly
 position. Failure to heed this warning could cause injury or death to friendly forces if
 inadvertent gun firing should occur.


   21.11.8. SOPGM Employment.
       21.11.8.1. A SOPGM engagement will normally be initiated by 9-line. All available
       crew members should record the information as it is received.
       21.11.8.2. Both the navigator and CSO will confirm the target.
       21.11.8.3. Any crew member recognizing an incorrect confirmation (friendly or target)
       or incorrect range/bearing will immediately advise the crew.
       21.11.8.4. Once the appropriate munition and conditions are determined, the navigator
       will state ―Pilot, Nav: Ripple #/Single, Weapon Type, Fuse, Maneuver, Run-In
       Restrictions (if required), Weapon Status.‖ Example: ―Pilot, Nav: Ripple 2 Griffins,
       PD, JHook, 120, 5 Minutes Remaining.‖
       21.11.8.5. The pilot will acknowledge the maneuver and any run-in restrictions.
       Example: ―Copy, JHook, 120.‖
       21.11.8.6. After the target data is entered into EM, the navigator will state ―Target Data
       Entered,‖ which initiates the STRIKE (SOPGM) checklist.
180                                               AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


      21.11.8.7. Based on EM inputs and the selected maneuver, the navigator will state
      ―Cleared To Maneuver‖ at the appropriate time in order to facilitate a successful
      engagement.
      21.11.8.8. Delaying or terminating SOPGM release.
         21.11.8.8.1. Prior to SOPGM release, any crew member observing a condition that
         could jeopardize a successful release/strike will call ―Hold Release, (Reason)‖ on the
         primary NET. The navigator will acknowledge the call. Switch positions will be as
         required.
         21.11.8.8.2. Prior to SOPGM release, any crew member observing a condition that
         could jeopardize safety will call ―Cease Fire‖ on the primary NET. The navigator
         and pilot will acknowledge the call. All switches will be placed to the SAFE
         position.
      21.11.8.9. After weapons impact or EM mission abort, all switches will be returned to
      their pre-launch condition. EXCEPTION: The Pilot‘s Master Arm switch may remain
      on.
  21.11.9. RESERVED FOR FUTURE USE.
  21.11.10. RESERVED FOR FUTURE USE.
  21.11.11. Poststrike Coordination.
      21.11.11.1. Run the POSTSTRIKE checklist upon completion of strike operations and
      prior to subsequent mobility events. If mission requirements dictate, the PIC may
      authorize the navigator to remain at the MOP during AAR.
      21.11.11.2. Crew Coordination.
         21.11.11.2.1. The copilot and FE will brief departure plan, aircraft configuration, and
         SCNS setup (control points, reference points, waypoints, and bullseyes) once the
         navigator is on the flight deck.
         21.11.11.2.2. The navigator will position to the flight deck and complete the
         navigation hand off with the copilot and the radar/defensive system set up with the
         FE.
         21.11.11.2.3. Flight Engineer. The FE will complete a radar and defensive system
         hand off with the navigator.
         21.11.11.2.4. Loadmaster. The LM will ensure that the gun is safed/stowed,
         SOPGMs are safed/secured, and resume scanning duties. When operating in a hostile
         environment, if the 30MM gun cannot be stowed electrically, corrective actions may
         be delayed until the ENROUTE or DESCENT checklist.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                               181


                                          Chapter 22

                          C130E/H EMPLOYMENT PROCEDURES

22.1. General. This chapter describes the specific equipment requirements, training restrictions,
and regulatory guidance for AFSOC-assigned, 6 SOS-allocated C130E/H Hercules tactical
employment. It is not a stand-alone reference and should be used in conjunction with AFTTP
33.MC130 and other directives. In the case of any conflict between AFTTP 33.MC130 and this
regulation, AFI 112MC130V3 takes precedence.
22.2. Minimum Operating Equipment. Refer to Table 22.1, Tactical Operations, for
minimum tactical equipment. Aircrew experience level and the mission scenario may dictate
greater equipment requirements than those listed. See paragraph 22.8 for degraded systems
procedures.

Table 22.1. Tactical Operations.
            Event                Radar     Minimum Navigation            Pilot’s     Navigator’s
                                                Solution1               RADALT        RADALT
 Day Low-level (LL)                                 I-DOP2                 X2
 NVG LL                             X2     I-INS or I-DOP & GPS2           X2             X2
 Threat Penetration                 X       I-INS or I-DOP & GPS           X              X
 VMC SCA                                             I-DOP                 X              X
           3
 IMC SCA                            X              GPS-INS                 X              X
 Covert NVG Landing                         I-INS or I-DOP & GPS           X              X
 Blackedout NVG Landing                     I-INS or I-DOP & GPS           X              X
 Airdrop                                             I-DOP                 X
 Notes:
 1. Navigator‘s IDCU must be operational.
 2. Aircraft that do not meet this requirement are restricted to MSA.
 3. Must have a GPS FOM of 4 or better on GPS position.
22.3. Minimum En route Altitudes and Visibility. Refer to Table 22.2, LL Operations
Minimum Altitude and Visibility. During training operations, higher weather, or altitude
minimums may be dictated by FLIP, ICAO procedures, host nation rules, or training
considerations.

Table 22.2. LL Operations Minimum Altitude and Visibility.
  Operation          Minimum             Minimum            Remarks/Limitations/Exceptions
                      Altitude            Visibility
 Day Visual         300 ft contour       Day VMC 1
 Night Un-             MSA 2             Night VMC         MSA may be segmented
 Aided
 NVG                500 ft contour         3 SM 1          All terrain types
 Threat              100 ft AGL          Day VMC 1         Limited to daytime coastal
 Penetration 3                                             penetration and flat to rolling terrain
 182                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


  Operation         Minimum               Minimum            Remarks/Limitations/Exceptions
                    Altitude              Visibility
 Notes:
 1. Pilots must be able to visually acquire controlling terrain at least 3 SM from the aircraft
 along the projected route of flight. Visual reference to the ground must be maintained and a
 visible horizon is recommended.
 2. Climbs must be initiated so that the aircraft is established at the higher MSA prior to the
 next segmented point. Descent to a lower MSA will not be initiated until past the next
 segmented point.
 3. A mission pilot must be in the left seat or a mission IP/EP in either seat.
 WARNING: Lack of sufficient illumination may prevent NVG contour operations in
 otherwise VMC conditions.

22.4. Degraded Systems Restrictions/Procedures. Degraded Systems Training (DST) is used
to prepare crews to accomplish the mission simulating certain pieces of equipment inoperative
(or intentionally turned off for emission control) during NVG low-level and airland operations.
Specifically, DST serves the purpose of training, via simulation of inoperative equipment, to
prepare for a possible lack of instrumentation aboard partner nation aircraft. Designate DST
route segments during the route brief. DST will not be performed by inexperienced crews, will
be taken into consideration during ORM assessment. The following restrictions and procedures
are designed to maximize training and safety.
   22.4.1. DST Specific Restrictions:
       22.4.1.1. PICs will brief the equipment simulated as inoperative, aircrew coordination,
       crew duties, and disorientation/emergency procedures prior to flight.
       22.4.1.2. Visibility must be at least 5 SM in mountainous terrain.
       22.4.1.3. Illumination: Effective illumination must be greater than 25% or DST must be
       conducted between the forecasted hours of sunset and estimated end of nautical twilight
       (EENT). Effective illumination must be greater than 50% if a safety observer is not
       aboard IAW paragraph 22.4.1.5 Otherwise, DST may be conducted between the
       forecasted hours of sunset and EENT.
       22.4.1.4. Equipment/Personnel Requirements:
          22.4.1.4.1. Radar Altimeter. The aircraft‘s radar altimeter must be operational and
          on during the entire flight. Additionally, both pilot and navigator indicators must be
          fully functioning.
          22.4.1.4.2. Ground Mapping/Weather Radar. The radar must be fully functional
          before flight. The navigator will function check the radar periodically during DST
          NVG low-level operations to verify functionality. If at any time during function
          check it is determined that the radar will not sufficiently provide terrain avoidance,
          DST operations will be discontinued. At this time tactical operations will revert to
          restrictions provided in Table 22.1.
          22.4.1.4.3. Navigation equipment. The SCNS INS or GPS must be operational and
          on during DST. A GPS moving map laptop with Portable Flight Planning System
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                                183


           (PFPS) and Digital Terrain Elevation Data (DTED) installed should be employed by
           the navigator. Chart coverage must meet the minimum requirements. DTED
           coverage will encompass the planned flight area and data within 25 NM of all course
           centerline.
       22.4.1.5. Personnel: A mission qualified instructor pilot must be at the controls. A
       mission instructor/evaluator navigator must be on board. The instructor/evaluator
       navigator, if not manning the navigator position, will be acting in an instructor/evaluator
       capacity. Restriction: During NVG low-level radar denied DST operation, a second
       mission qualified navigator (or mission qualified pilot) will act as the safety observer, and
       will monitor the progress of the mission using NVGs. The scanner will wear NVGs and
       carry an additional chart. Restriction: During NVG low-level radar denied DST
       operation, a second mission qualified navigator (or mission qualified pilot) will act as the
       safety observer, and will monitor the progress of the mission using NVGs. The scanner
       will wear NVGs and carry an additional chart prepared.
   22.4.2. Procedures:
       22.4.2.1. Single Radar Altimeter operations. For single radar altimeter operations the
       navigator‘s radar altimeter repeater only will be placed to off and the flight engineer will
       assume all responsibilities for radar altimeter altitude calls referencing the pilot‘s radar
       altimeter as directed by the PIC. Equipment/Personnel requirements must be met IAW
       paragraph 22.4.1.4
       22.4.2.2. Radar denied operations. The radar will be placed in the standby position
       during NVG low-level, radar denied operations in order to simulate an
       inoperative/unavailable radar. A safety observer will be on board IAW paragraph
       22.4.1.5 Having the radar in standby will allow a quick transition to normal NVG low-
       level procedures during limited visibility or in emergency situations (inadvertent weather
       penetration, emergency climb, etc.). The navigator will turn on the radar any time cloud
       cover degrades effective illumination in flight. Equipment/Personnel requirements must
       be met IAW paragraph 22.4.1.4
       22.4.2.3. Simulated SCNS denied/degraded operations.                 Simulated SCNS
       denial/degradation maybe accomplished during any phase of flight regardless of the
       illumination. The flight director mode switches can be positioned to simulate the INS
       and/or GPS inoperative or the navigator may select a degraded SCNS submode via their
       IDCU. Additionally, any crew position IDCU may be dimmed. The crews primary
       navigational references become the copilots/navigator‘s charts and moving map PFPS. If
       any crew member becomes disoriented an immediate climb to ESA will be initiated IAW.
       Equipment/Personnel requirements must be met IAW paragraph 22.4.1.4
22.5. NVG Low-level Operational Risk Management. Risk mitigation for low illumination
includes detailed recommended altitudes and an analysis of cultural lighting. If necessary, limit
the flight to flat terrain. Be prepared to explain to the Squadron CC/DO how these were used.
22.6. En route Operations.
   22.6.1. Pilot Charts. Copilots are required to carry a navigational chart (minimum of TPC in
   scale) during NVG LL operations. The pilot and copilot may share a single low-level chart
   during flight. This chart will be prepared to the same requirements as navigator/EWO charts.
 184                                                 AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


   22.6.2. Fuel Planning. For tactical low-level operations, assume a 6,000 pound per hour
   burn rate unless more accurate data is available.
   22.6.3. Planned Groundspeeds. Normally plan low-level operations at a groundspeed of 230
   knots. In situations of heavy aircraft gross weight or where strong headwinds prevail
   consider using a groundspeed of 220 knots. When flying low-level in high density altitude
   situations normally plan to use a groundspeed of 240-250 knots. NOTE: Regardless of the
   radar altimeter setting you use, if you are below the desired modified contour altitude, a
   correction to altitude should be made. Do not wait for the low light to illuminate to begin a
   correction. This is especially important when using the lower limit on the radar altimeter.
   22.6.4. Tower Avoidance. Any tower or man-made obstruction within 3 NM of planned
   flight path must be positively identified visually or on radar. If not identified by 3 NM prior
   to the tower or obstacle, climb to at least 300 ft above the obstruction until clear.
   22.6.5. Pilot Seat Swaps. Climb to MSA when either pilot must leave the seat during low-
   level flight. During planned seat swaps, when a qualified instructor remains in the seat the
   crew may continue low-level flight or climb to a safe altitude.
   22.6.6. Radar Altimeter Low Lights. Any crew member noting illumination of the low
   warning light on the radar altimeter will call ―Low‖ to indicate this to the PF. WARNING:
   Lack of immediate action could result in controlled flight into terrain (CFIT).
   22.6.7. Altimeter Updating: Ensure that the radar altimeter is fully operational and use
   sound judgment when updating aircraft altimeters
   22.6.8. The navigator will provide the new magnetic course and MSA prior to the turn point.
   The navigator will brief anticipated threats and aircraft deviations for the next leg. The
   navigator and pilots share the responsibility of clearing terrain in the direction of turn, and no
   other duties must be allowed to interfere with this primary responsibility.
22.7. SCA Procedures.
   22.7.1. SCA Procedures. SCAs may be used for approaches to conventional airfields and
   assault zones, and for either overt or NVG airland operations.
       22.7.1.1. Chart construction will be IAW Chapter 11 and Chapter 17.
       22.7.1.2. SCA Weather minimums:
          22.7.1.2.1. For NIMA-published SCAs, use published minimums but no lower than
          300 ft ceiling and 1 SM visibility.
       22.7.1.3. Pilot/Copilot Landing Procedures. The SCA may either be flown and landed
       by a single pilot or flown by one and landed by the other. This distinction will be made
       during the ENROUTE portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist. In some situations (i.e.,
       low visibility or low illumination), it may be more advantageous for the copilot to fly the
       SCA, and for the pilot to take control of the aircraft for landing. This allows the landing
       pilot to visually search for the LZ while the copilot‘s focus is on flying the SCA.
          22.7.1.3.1. During NVG operations, an NVG landing qualified mission pilot (non-
          instructor) may fly the SCA, and takeoff and land from the right seat provided an
          NVG landing qualified pilot is also in the left seat. Copilots, if certified, or in
          upgrade, may land and takeoff from the right seat using only normal NVG procedures
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                             185


         provided that a NVG airland current and qualified PIC occupies the left seat and the
         runway width is 100 ft or greater.
         22.7.1.3.2. If the pilot and copilot positions are manned by NVG airland qualified
         MP/IP, it is allowable for the pilot to fly the SCA, and for the copilot to take control
         of the aircraft for landing. For approaches and landings not requiring NVGs (day
         operations or overt night operations to lighted runways) either pilot or copilot may fly
         the approach and accomplish the landing.
         22.7.1.3.3. Mission copilots will not descend below MDA and will maintain the
         MDA until the pilot takes control or until reaching the missed approach point.
         Execute a go-around if the runway environment is not in sight at the MAP, directed,
         or a safe landing cannot be made. A mission copilot may perform the NVG go-
         around; however, if an immediate landing feasible is attempted they will not descend
         below MDA and will maintain the MDA until the pilot takes control or until reaching
         the missed approach point unless authorized to land.
         22.7.1.3.4. See Chapter 5 for maximum effort takeoff and landing guidance and
         policy.
     22.7.1.4. All infil/exfil time advisories and respective actions will be conducted IAW
     Chapter 17 of this publication.
     22.7.1.5. VMC SCA. Do not descend below MDA until the touchdown zone is visually
     identified and confirmed by both pilots.
     22.7.1.6. IMC SCA. Do not descend below MDA ft until LZ environment is visually
     identified and confirmed by both pilots.
  22.7.2. SCA Coordination. During training missions with no onboard user involved, the PIC
  will determine and brief what time warnings (60-, 30-, 20-10-, 6-, and 1-minute) are required
  for the mission to be flown. The pilot and navigator will brief the SCA using the appropriate
  aircrew mission briefing guide during the ENROUTE portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist
  or the crew briefing step of the DESCENT checklist. Additional briefing items may be
  added, as required, for the particular mission. NOTE: During training missions, timed
  warnings may be compressed with user concurrence. The following infil/exfil procedures
  apply:
     22.7.2.1. Sixty Minutes Prior to Landing.
         22.7.2.1.1. When required, the navigator will alert the crew by stating, ―Crew, 60-
         Minute Advisory.‖ The loadmaster will acknowledge by stating ―Acknowledged,
         Loadmaster.‖ The loadmaster will notify the troop commander, check the load
         restraint, secure loose equipment, position or don required survival and emergency
         equipment, check NVGs, install and secure canary slide or type IV ground loading
         ramps, and set cargo compartment lighting for NVG operations.
     22.7.2.2. Thirty Minutes Prior to Landing.
         22.7.2.2.1. When required, the navigator will alert the crew by stating, ―Crew, 30-
         Minute Advisory.‖ The loadmaster will acknowledge by stating ―Acknowledged,
         Loadmaster.‖
186                                                 AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


         22.7.2.2.2. The loadmaster will notify the troop commander and activate emergency
         exit chemical lights.
      22.7.2.3. Twenty Minutes Prior to Landing.
         22.7.2.3.1. When required, the navigator will alert the crew by stating, ―Crew, 20-
         Minute Warning.‖ The loadmaster will acknowledge by stating ―Acknowledged,
         Loadmaster.‖
         22.7.2.3.2. The loadmaster will notify the troop commander.
         22.7.2.3.3. The pilot will call for the ENROUTE portion of the INFIL/EXFIL
         checklist (this may be done earlier at the pilot's discretion) and configure NVG
         lighting. If normal checklists are being used, call for the DESCENT checklist.
         22.7.2.3.4. The navigator will determine the most accurate QNH altimeter setting for
         the LZ using all available sources including GPS, weather forecasts, ground station
         reporting, and altitude calibration, as available. If unsure of the most accurate
         altimeter setting, the altimeters will be set at the lowest altimeter setting for the route
         or route segment.
         22.7.2.3.5. The flight engineer will close the blackout curtains and install eyebrow
         window curtains (if required), and all crew members will set lighting for NVG
         operations.
         22.7.2.3.6. The pilots will don NVGs. The flight engineer will have NVGs ready for
         immediate use. The pilots will visually maintain terrain clearance and will clear for
         obstacles and other traffic. The pilots will cross-check airspeeds, altitudes,
         configuration, and headings, and will check for light bleed-through.
         22.7.2.3.7. The flight engineer will monitor aircraft systems and cross-check
         airspeeds, altitudes, headings, and configuration.
      22.7.2.4. Ten Minutes Prior to Landing.
         22.7.2.4.1. When required, the navigator will alert the crew by stating, ―Crew, 10-
         Minute Warning.‖ The loadmaster will acknowledge by stating ―Acknowledged,
         Loadmaster.‖
         22.7.2.4.2. The loadmaster will notify the troop commander, disconnect/disengage
         ADS arms, remove canary slide safety locks (if installed), and check NVGs.
      22.7.2.5. Six Minutes Prior to Landing.
         22.7.2.5.1. The loadmaster will relay the six-minute advisory to the onboard user.
         This is normally silent to the rest of the crew.
         22.7.2.5.2. The loadmaster will position and secure personnel, set cargo compartment
         lights as required for landing, and don NVGs if required.
      22.7.2.6. At the Initial Point.
         22.7.2.6.1. After visual acquisition of the initial point, terrain permitting, the
         navigator will sequence steering to the run-in leg so as to roll out on final course
         guidance without overshooting.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                              187


        22.7.2.6.2. The navigator will make distance calls at every mile inside ten miles and
        every half mile inside three miles (or as briefed). The navigator will announce
        distance to go advisories in conjunction with course advisory information.
     22.7.2.7. Slowdown Procedures.
        22.7.2.7.1. Approximately 3 NM prior to the planned glide slope intercept point, the
        navigator will initiate the slowdown by stating ―Slowdown Now.‖
        22.7.2.7.2. The pilot flying will slow the aircraft to no less than normal 50%
        threshold speed, configure the flaps and gear as airspeed permits, and call for the
        appropriate checklist (BEFORE LANDING portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist or
        normal BEFORE LANDING checklist). He will call for 100% flaps as airspeed
        permits, slow to normal or maximum effort threshold speed. NOTE: Do not slow
        below 50% flap normal threshold speed until flaps are tracking to 100%.
        22.7.2.7.3. The engineer will advise the copilot as soon as the landing ―Gear Is
        Down‖ and standby for clearance to set the flaps to 100%. Upon receiving clearance
        from the PF, the engineer will state ―Tracking‖ and resume the INFIL/EXFIL
        checklist.
        22.7.2.7.4. Radar altimeters will remain set at en route altitude setting until the
        slowdown. The pilot will set the radar altimeter to the pre-briefed setting when called
        for in the checklist. A 25 or 10 ft setting on the pilot‘s radar altimeter helps judge the
        last few ft above the runway. The navigator should set 300 ft or as briefed to guard
        against shallow glide slopes and to prepare for a go-around and climb-out.
        22.7.2.7.5. Once the slowdown is initiated, no further airspeed corrections for TOA
        control should be made. NOTE: When slowdowns are initiated further out than 3
        NM prior to glide slope intercept, consider maintaining 140 KCAS (or approach
        speed, whichever is higher) and delaying the call for 100% flaps until 1 NM prior to
        glide slope intercept.
     22.7.2.8. One Mile to Glide Slope Intercept.
        22.7.2.8.1. Approximately 1 NM prior to glide slope intercept, the navigator will
        state "Expect Descent In One Mile." Approaching glide slope intercept, the navigator
        will confirm the accuracy of the barometric altimeter setting by comparing actual
        AGL and MSL altitudes with those depicted on the SCA template.
        22.7.2.8.2. If not already accomplished, the pilot flying will call for 100% flaps.
        22.7.2.8.3. The flight engineer will check gear down and flaps set to 100%.
     22.7.2.9. Glide Slope Intercept.
        22.7.2.9.1. At glide slope intercept, the navigator will state ―Begin Descent‖ and the
        desired initial descent rate. Approaching glide slope intercept, the navigator will
        confirm the accuracy of the barometric altimeter setting by comparing actual AGL
        and MSL altitudes with those depicted on the SCA template. The pilot flying will
        begin descent by setting and monitoring the planned/stated VVI.
        22.7.2.9.2. The navigator will monitor glide slope deviation. Report deviations
        greater than 50 ft, along with an advisory as to whether the aircraft is correcting to,
188                                               AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


         paralleling, or diverging from glide path. Whoever identifies the LZ first will call out
         ―Zone In Sight‖ with clock position and any discrepancies noted (i.e., any lights out,
         etc.). Both pilots will then confirm the zone and cross-check the alignment for the
         proper heading.       WARNING: The navigator will verify that the actual aircraft
         position is inside the planned SCA obstacle template prior to initiating descent.
         22.7.2.9.3. The loadmaster will notify the onboard user of the 1 minute warning
         when the navigator calls ―2 Miles.‖ WARNING: Use of a 5-degree glide slope can
         cause descent rates in excess of 1,000 ft per minute. Extreme care must be taken to
         break high descent rates with application of power prior to touchdown.
         WARNING: Do not slow to threshold speed until established on final.
         22.7.2.9.4. The navigator, backed up by the pilot monitoring, will advise the pilot
         flying at 100 ft above the MDA and at MDA. The pilot flying will acknowledge the
         call and level the aircraft at MDA if the runway is not in sight. The pilot flying will
         maintain the MDA until the runway is in sight or until reaching the MAP (0 nautical
         miles to go to runway threshold on the SCNS).
         22.7.2.9.5. Visual Acquisition of the LZ.
         22.7.2.9.6. The pilot monitoring may direct the pilot flying to the LZ (remain at or
         above MDA). The pilot monitoring will ensure that the pilot flying and navigator
         understand that he has taken over the responsibility for directing the aircraft. After
         positively identifying the touchdown point, the pilot flying will accomplish the
         landing. In some situations, it may be more advantageous to have the other pilot take
         control of the aircraft for landing.
         22.7.2.9.7. The flight engineer will assist in field acquisition and visually check that
         the LZ is clear of obstacles.
         22.7.2.9.8. The pilot monitoring will monitor airspeed, descent rate, and bank angle
         and will advise the pilot flying if descent rate exceeds 1,000 FPM (or as briefed for
         steep glide slopes) or if bank angle exceeds 15 degrees. The navigator will provide
         the pilot with 100, 50, 25, and 10 ft height-above-touchdown calls. The copilot may
         provide the pilot with airspeed and descent rate information if requested by the pilot.
         Intersperse this information with the HAT calls from the navigator.
         22.7.2.9.9. When the navigator calls 100 ft, the loadmaster will notify the onboard
         user, ―Prepare To Land.‖
      22.7.2.10. After Touchdown.
         22.7.2.10.1. The loadmaster may open the ramp and door when throttles have been
         moved to reverse or as otherwise briefed.
         22.7.2.10.2. Upon landing, the copilot can provide airspeed to the pilot until slowing
         to 60 KIAS. These calls are optional. The copilot will have NVGs focused and ready
         for immediate use during taxi operations and assist in locating the briefed location.
         The copilot and flight engineer will assist the pilot in locating the off-load/onload
         point once taxi speed is reached, and clear for other aircraft and obstructions.
         22.7.2.10.3. When the aircraft is stopped at the off-load/onload location, the pilot
         will give the loadmaster clearance by stating ―Clear To Off-load/Onload.‖ Do not
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                             189


        move the aircraft after off-load/onload has commenced until the loadmaster calls
        ―Cleared To Taxi.‖ When there is a delay in the off-load/onload or an extended
        ground time is anticipated, raise the ramp approximately 12 inches above the ground
        so the aircraft can taxi in the event of an emergency. Refer to Attachment 2 and
        AFTTP 3-3 MC130, for further guidance.
        22.7.2.10.4. Touch and go operations are authorized during infil/exfil operations with
        or without the use of NVGs. Complete the BEFORE LANDING portion of the
        INFIL/EXFIL checklist on the initial approach to the runway and transition to the ON
        THE RUNWAY touch and go steps (asterisked) of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist after
        touchdown. After the first touch and go, asterisked of the INFIL/EXFIL checklists
        may be used. The crew will continue using normal SCA approach and landing
        procedures. The navigator may make calls as briefed by the PIC during visual
        patterns when established on downwind and the AFTER TAKEOFF touch and go
        steps of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist are complete. Time warnings are not required.
        Complete the full INFIL/EXFIL AFTER TAKEOFF checklist on the last departure
        from the airfield. Use normal checklist procedures IAW T.O. 1C130(E)E-1, Section
        II, when not using the INFIL/EXFIL checklist.
     22.7.2.11. Go-around. Execute a go-around if the runway is not in sight at the MAP or if
     a safe landing cannot be made.
        22.7.2.11.1. The navigator will advise the pilots upon arriving at the MAP and repeat
        MSL altitude, the initial heading, and will call altitudes as specified climbing through
        from 50 ft to 200 ft AGL (i.e., 50, 100, 150, 200) if a go-around is executed.
        22.7.2.11.2. If landing is desired, the crew may fly a visual (closed) pattern back to
        landing (Immediate Landing Feasible), or they may proceed to the IP for another
        approach.
     22.7.2.12. Immediate Landing Feasible. The pilot will state ―Go-around, Immediate
     Landing Feasible.‖ After establishing a safe climb, the pilot flying will direct the flaps
     set to 50%, and to leave the landing gear down and visually fly back to the runway. The
     pilot flying will call for 100% flaps before or after rolling off the perch and call for the
     INFIL/EXFIL BEFORE LANDING checklist. The engineer will pick up the checklist by
     reconfirming the landing gear position and accomplishing the remaining items of the
     checklist. The pilot will maintain 140 KIAS or approach speed, whichever is higher until
     rolling out on final.
        22.7.2.12.1. This maneuver should only require the movement of flaps and landing
        light switches. If any other change in configuration is accomplished, then the entire
        INFIL/EXFIL checklist will be used.
        22.7.2.12.2. The aircrew will route study all landing zones to ensure how an
        immediate landing feasible will be performed to each.
     22.7.2.13. Loss of NVGs:
        22.7.2.13.1. Airborne. Unless otherwise briefed, if the pilot or copilot loses use of
        their NVGs inside of 1 NM, perform a go-around. Instructor qualified pilots may
        continue to land IAW Chapter 17. If the flight engineer loses use of NVGs, land at
 190                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


          the discretion of the PIC. The other crew members must increase their clearing duties
          and confirm the zone. After takeoff, continue the climb out and follow the
          appropriate procedures for loss of NVGs.
          22.7.2.13.2. On the Ground. The pilot will determine whether to continue the
          landing rollout or takeoff roll as applicable. The copilot should be prepared to turn on
          overt lighting at the direction of the PIC in case of NVG failure during takeoff or
          landing roll.
       22.7.2.14. Takeoff Procedures. Also see Chapter 5.
          22.7.2.14.1. At takeoff speed, the pilot will rotate the aircraft to establish a positive
          climb rate (normally, this will be approximately a 7-degree nose-up attitude). The
          copilot checks the pitch attitude and will raise the landing gear on the command of
          the pilot after ensuring a positive rate of climb. The flight engineer will back up the
          pilot using NVGs during climb out. The copilot may take control of the aircraft if
          directed by the pilot, and continue the instrument climb out to the egress route
          altitude. The pilot flying directs the accomplishment of the AFTER TAKEOFF
          portion of the INFIL/EXFIL checklist, the AFTER TAKEOFF touch and go steps of
          the INFIL/EXFIL checklist, or the normal AFTER TAKEOFF checklist, depending
          on the scenario.
          22.7.2.14.2. As soon as conditions permit, the loadmaster will check equipment
          restraint, and check condition and needs of the onboard user.
22.8. Airdrop Operations.
   22.8.1. On personnel airdrops where surface winds are unknown, (such as unmanned DZs)
   advise the jumpmaster and Army airborne mission commander when drop altitude winds
   exceed 30 knots. In this instance, the decision to drop is at user's discretion.
   22.8.2. Drop zone (DZ) Survey Review. Both pilots and the navigator will review the DZ
   survey during mission planning. Memorize PI location, DZ dimensions, 10-second warning
   location, airdrop safety box, and prominent DZ features.
   22.8.3. The Radar Altimeter will be set to 50 ft below planned drop altitude.
   22.8.4. SCNS Airdrop/Troop Jump Computer-Manual Select Switch shall be set to AD/TJ
   manual.
   22.8.5. A maximum of ten static lines may be retrieved manually per paratroop door. A
   maximum of 20 static lines per cable may be retrieved with a static line retriever winch.
   22.8.6. Two loadmasters are required, except for SATB. At least one loadmaster should
   remain on interphone at all times.
   22.8.7. Navigating to the Release Point:
       22.8.7.1. Pilot and Navigator Release Point Procedures. To position the aircraft
       accurately at the release point, use the following pilot/navigator procedures:
          22.8.7.1.1. Airspeed adjustments should be completed prior to the IP or slowdown,
          whichever occurs first.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                               191


         22.8.7.1.2. If the IP is visible from a distance and time control permits, overfly the IP
         on run-in heading. Otherwise, fly a curved path planned leg in order to rollout on
         course to the DZ.
         22.8.7.1.3. After departing the IP, the pilot not flying the aircraft will assist the
         navigator in maintaining the desired track inbound by confirming aircraft position
         with the planned run-in update points.
         22.8.7.1.4. The navigator provides headings that will position the aircraft the
         required distance upwind from centerline track so that large corrections will not be
         required on the final approach to the release point.
  22.8.8. Slowdown Procedures. During airdrop operations, the pilot will perform the
  slowdown maneuver and accomplish the SLOWDOWN checklist at a predetermined point
  prior to the release point. The preselected slowdown point will be an easily identifiable
  geographical point at or prior to the distance specified for the slowdown. A computer
  position or pre-briefed timing procedure may be used when a geographical point is not
  available. Begin slowdown at a distance commensurate with proficiency of crew. Decrease
  slowdown distance as proficiency increases. The objective is to reduce the time the aircraft
  is vulnerable at drop altitude and airspeed. After the navigator calls for the slowdown:
     22.8.8.1. The pilot retards the throttles to flight idle (0-1,000 inch pounds of torque) and
     calls for ―Flaps 50%, On Speed.‖
     22.8.8.2. The flight engineer lowers the flaps in 10% increments on speed.
     22.8.8.3. The copilot opens the air deflector doors (if required) upon slowing below 150
     KIAS. For tailgate drops, the flight engineer calls ―Ramp And Door‖ as part of the
     checklist, the loadmaster followed by the pilot state ―Clear To Open,‖ and the flight
     engineer opens the cargo door and ramp.        WARNING: For personnel airdrops, the
     pilot must be aware of paratroopers standing in the back and avoid drastic pitch or bank
     changes after the 6-Minute Advisory. NOTE: For CDS drops, reset the flaps IAW the
     CDS flap setting chart in the abbreviated checklist only.
     22.8.8.4. At slowdown, immediately double the drift used prior to slowdown and apply it
     to the run-in heading. This will keep the aircraft on the upwind side of the PI.
     22.8.8.5. The navigator informs the pilot of the expected drift, the heading required to
     parallel the DZ axis, and confirms the drop altitude and airspeed.
     22.8.8.6. As soon as the DZ is visible and identified by the pilot and navigator, they
     jointly confirm the release point location, lateral offset, and track required. The first crew
     member to identify the pre-briefed DZ markings will state ―PI IN SIGHT‖. The second
     crew member to identify the markings will state ―PI IN SIGHT, XX O‘CLOCK‖ (PI
     location clock position). The pilot then assumes the responsibility for maintaining the
     offset distance and required track. The navigator will pick the timing point, if required,
     control the green light and red light time and continually cross-check the offset distance.
     22.8.8.7. During the final seconds of the approach to the release point, the pilot not
     flying the aircraft places a hand near the jump light switch. 10 seconds prior to release,
     the navigator will give a preparatory call of ―10 Seconds.‖ At this call, if the aircraft
     location is not within pre-coordinated parameters, a ―No Drop‖ condition exists.
192                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


  22.8.9. During the drop:
      22.8.9.1. The loadmaster will not deploy equipment or personnel until hearing and
      seeing the green light.
      22.8.9.2. The pilot will make any small corrections required to maintain the track.
  22.8.10. Departure From Drop Zone (Escape). The navigator will call ―Red Light‖ at the
  expiration of the ―Green Light‖ time or upon hearing the loadmaster‘s call of ―Load Clear‖,
  whichever occurs first. When the red light is turned on crews may immediately begin the
  escape maneuver by tracking flaps to 50% if required, turning to the departure heading,
  increase airspeed to 140 KIAS, and attain en route altitude. To facilitate static line retrieval
  and preclude entanglement, do not exceed 140 KIAS until the static lines are retrieved.
  During combat, static lines that cannot be retrieved will be cut so that doors can be closed.
  Close all doors, raise flaps, and accelerate to en route airspeed. It is imperative that the
  aircraft be configured for high-speed evasive maneuvers. Therefore, after the static lines
  have been retrieved or cut, the doors and air deflectors closed, and flaps raised, the remainder
  of the COMPLETION OF DROP checklist may be deferred to a more convenient time while
  returning to the recovery base.
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                               193


                                           Chapter 23

                PSYCHOLOGICAL OPERATIONS/LEAFLET AIRDROP

23.1. General. Leaflet airdrops are accomplished in both hostile and permissive environments.
23.2. Mission Description. Mission requirements vary widely depending on the volume of
leaflets airdropped, leaflet packaging, and the altitude of the airdrop. Loads will normally not
exceed 25,000 pounds. Drop altitudes may vary from 500 ft AGL to the aircraft service ceiling.
Leaflet drops require the movement of boxes of various weights from throughout the cargo
compartment via intermediate rollers (if required) to the ramp area for dispersal off the ramp. If
specific mission requirements dictate deviation from information contained in this publication,
modify procedures to meet specific requirements. NOTE: For all airdrops conducted above
FL250 comply with AFI 11202V3. Long duration high altitude drops will require the aircraft
remain open. Consider outside air temperature and clothing requirements prior to step.
23.3. Equipment. In addition to normal aircraft equipment, the following items may be
required to perform leaflet drops:
   23.3.1. Parachutes or restraining harnesses must be available for use by all personnel
   working in the cargo compartment. Normally six are required.
   23.3.2. A portable oxygen console will be used IAW AFI 11409.
   23.3.3. An interval timer is desirable (alternating red and green jump lights may serve for
   sequencing the drops).
23.4. Aircraft Configuration. Determine if leaflets are floor loaded (on aircraft dual rail
intermediate conveyers) or are configured on pallets. Based on how the leaflets are prepared and
the volume of leaflets to airdrop, there are several possible configurations.
   23.4.1. Palletized Loads. Preload large volumes of leaflets on pallets and load as palletized
   cargo. Resources permitting, this is the recommended procedure for large volumes of
   leaflets.
       23.4.1.1. Use modified pallets with warehouse rollers attached to the top of the pallets in
       two sticks.
       23.4.1.2. Stack the leaflet boxes on the pallets in two sticks and secure them to the
       pallets before loading aircraft.
       23.4.1.3. In addition to the modified pallets, provide a bridge to span the gap between the
       last floor pallet and the ramp pallet.
           23.4.1.3.1. Construct the bridge so the rollers on it are the same height as the rollers
           on top of the pallets when loaded on the aircraft dual rails (MC/C130).
           23.4.1.3.2. Install the bridge after the ramp is lowered to the airdrop position and
           before completion of the slowdown or 3-minute checklist.
   23.4.2. Floor Loads. Leaflets may also be loaded directly on the aircraft intermediate
   conveyors, if available. Employ this procedure for smaller loads of leaflets or when the
   equipment mentioned above is not available.
 194                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


       23.4.2.1. Individual boxes have to be hand loaded and each group of boxes individually
       secured using aircraft tie-down equipment.
       23.4.2.2. Configure the aircraft IAW the applicable volume and addenda.
          23.4.2.2.1. Recommend the intermediate conveyors be moved inboard to the high-
          speed configuration (MC130E/H/W aircraft), if size of the load permits.
          23.4.2.2.2. If the intermediate conveyors must remain in the outboard configuration,
          exercise care during loading to ensure the individual boxes contact two intermediate
          rollers. The boxes may not roll or may be damaged if loaded on only one roller
          conveyor.
              23.4.2.2.2.1. If the intermediate conveyors must remain in the outboard
              configuration, consider using CDS skid boards or sheets of plywood as skids
              under the leaflet boxes.
              23.4.2.2.2.2. If using this option, secure each skid of leaflets individually using
              aircraft tie-down equipment.
              23.4.2.2.2.3. Drift each skid back to the ramp hinge during the airdrop, exercising
              caution not to let the skid board exit the aircraft.
              23.4.2.2.2.4. Secure the empty skids against the side wall until the airdrop is
              completed.
23.5. Preparation for Loading. Complete applicable portions of the appropriate T.O.
1C130A9 checklists as follows:
   23.5.1. Cargo Loading Preparation.
   23.5.2. Cargo Loading (Palletized/Floor Load).
   23.5.3. General Airplane Preparation for Ramp and Door Airdrop/Extraction.
   23.5.4. Anchor Cables. Rig one anchor cable on each side of the aircraft.
       23.5.4.1. Attach the aft latch of each anchor cable to the inboard U-bolt on the aft anchor
       cable support on each side of the airplane.
       23.5.4.2. Position the anchor cable stops to the very forward or aft end of the cable, or
       remove and stow the stops, unless they are used as stops for the restraint harness restraint
       strap.
       23.5.4.3. Attach the forward latch to the center U-bolts on the forward anchor cable
       support.
          23.5.4.3.1. If the center U-bolts are not accessible because of aircraft configuration,
          attach the forward latch to the outboard U-bolts.
          23.5.4.3.2. If connecting the forward latch to the outboard U-bolts, leave additional
          slack in the cable to allow anchor cable installation in the outboard location on the A-
          frame during the airdrop.
          23.5.4.3.3. Lower the center anchor cable supports and lock them in the vertical
          position with the tubular supports. Do not connect the anchor cable to the A-frame
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                              195


           during rigging. The cable may block the cargo door from properly opening and
           closing.
           23.5.4.3.4. The aft anchor cable support arm micro switches should be isolated so
           that the cargo ramp will operate with the anchor cables lowered.
              23.5.4.3.4.1. Isolate the micro switches by securing them back with 1/4-inch
              cotton webbing or gutted Type III nylon.
              23.5.4.3.4.2. Test the ramp for operation with the micro switches secured and the
              anchor cables lowered to ensure the ties were properly installed.
   23.5.5. Static line Retrievers. Suspend oxygen hoses from static line retriever cables for
   high altitude missions (not required if airdropping below 10,000 ft MSL).
       23.5.5.1. Unwind static line retriever cable(s).
       23.5.5.2. Install quick disconnects on the aft end of cable(s).
       23.5.5.3. Secure the quick disconnects to the inboard side of the A-frame with two turns
       of 1/4-inch cotton webbing. Make this tie approximately 18 inches above the anchor
       cable attaching point. If the static line retriever is inadvertently rewound, the tie will
       break and will prevent damage to the A-frame or static line retriever.
       23.5.5.4. Remove all slack from static line retriever cables.
   23.5.6. Oxygen Hoses and Interphone Cord. Tape or tie extended oxygen hoses and
   interphone cords together and suspend from static line retriever cables with G-13 clevis or
   suitable substitute.
       23.5.6.1. Ensure that approximately 12 G-13 clevises or suitable substitutes are available
       to tie oxygen hoses to the static line retriever cable(s).
       23.5.6.2. Recommend the kicker use the oxygen regulator and interphone cord aft of the
       left paratroop door. Route the cord and hose up and over the overhead structural beams,
       and back down next to the overhead ADS release. This technique keeps the hose and
       cord off the floor preventing them from becoming tangled or damaged. It also keeps it
       out of the kicker's way while kicking boxes and retrieving static lines.
23.6. Securing Boxes. Secure five boxes on the ramp for airdrop. If loading preloaded pallets,
two sticks of five boxes each, are secured on the ramp pallet.
23.7. Connecting Static Lines. Connect static lines to the anchor cable aft of the A-frame.
23.8. Restraint Strapping. Place a single length of Type III nylon, as final restraint, over the
stick of boxes. The cargo restraint strap over the boxes on the ramp is removed at the 1-minute
warning. The Type III nylon final restraint is cut at the release point.
 196                                                 AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


Figure 23.1. Leaflet Box Rigging Procedures.




23.9. Leaflet Box Rigging Procedures. Rigging boxes for high-altitude static line
dissemination.
   23.9.1. Using a punch, cut four holes approximately 2 1/2 inches by 3/4 inches as indicated
   in Figure 21.1 If a punch is not available, a knife can be used. When using a punch, the box
   must first be placed upon an object slightly deeper and smaller than the box so that a hard
   surface is provided when striking the punch.
   23.9.2. Cut through the box from one hole to another as illustrated in Figure 23.1
   23.9.3. Place a 2-inch wide length of webbing through the holes. The length of webbing
   depends upon the size of the box, but must be long enough to tie after the box has been filled
   with leaflets.
   23.9.4. Place a 3- or 4-inch strip of masking tape over the 2-inch wide webbing.
   23.9.5. Cut all four corners of the box from the top to about three-fourths of the way down
   (or less).
   23.9.6. Again using masking tape, tape one strip up the cut portion and two across, as
   illustrated.
   23.9.7. After filling the box with leaflets, tie the two strips of webbing.
   23.9.8. Attach one end of the static line to the webbing ties. The length of the static line
   depends upon the type of aircraft, but it is normally about 15 ft long. The box is rolled out of
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                                  197


   the aircraft and as the container comes to the end of the static, line, the sides of the box split.
   In effect, it is turned inside out. The leaflets fall away followed by the empty box.
   23.9.9. This is one method, and may not be the method suited for your mission. Other
   variations do exist; however, this is the most universal and least complex.
23.10. Preparation for Airdrop. After takeoff, take the following actions before starting the
leaflet airdrop checklist.
   23.10.1. Tape all ramp control panel switches, except the auxiliary pump and ramp switch.
   23.10.2. Lower anchor cables and connect them to the outboard side of the anchor cable
   supports.
   23.10.3. Safety tie the aft anchor cable support micro switches.
   23.10.4. Position/don emergency equipment and survival gear.
   23.10.5. Begin Leaflet Airdrop checklist.
23.11. Aircrew Procedures. Under normal circumstances the crew required for leaflet
operations consists of a tactical crew plus two PTs (for high altitude airdrops) and three
additional crew members to act as feeders. The senior loadmaster will assign specific positions
for activity in the cargo compartment.
   23.11.1. Drop Configuration/Maneuvering. Base configuration on desired coverage and
   airframe airspeed limitations. All personnel should be constantly aware of the possibility of
   an emergency break upon encountering a threat.
       23.11.1.1. Flaps. Flaps are normally set at 50% and flap settings should not be changed
       during the drop unless the loadmaster is notified. NOTE: At very high altitudes, the
       aircraft may not be able to maintain drop airspeed with 50% flaps. Under these
       conditions, use an intermediate flap setting that allows for aircraft performance and also
       produces an acceptable deck angle for the drop. A flap setting near 20% gives the best
       aircraft wing performance while providing high rudder boost for controllability.
       23.11.1.2. Airspeed. Base KIAS/KCAS on desired groundspeed. This may range from
       125 to 150 KIAS/KCAS. Other speed ranges may apply if not dropping off the ramp
       (i.e., cargo door only).
23.12. Static Lines. To increase speed of closing the ramp if a hostile threat is detected and to
prevent damage to the aircraft, the static lines should be stowed after each box goes out. Do this
if the interval between boxes permits safe operation. Static lines may be cut in an emergency.
 198                                                 AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


                                           Chapter 24

                          SEARCH AND RESCUE PROCEDURES

24.1. General. Most searches are part of an effort to save life; therefore, make every effort to
complete the search as rapidly and efficiently as possible. A thorough scan of the search area
and accurate navigation significantly increase the probability of detection. Reactions to sightings
must be timely and accurate. This section outlines operational procedures for conducting
effective searches in the C130 aircraft. Refer to the International Aeronautical and Maritime
Search and Rescue Manual (IAMSAR) Volumes I/II for additional information.

24.2. Search Altitude. Select an optimum search altitude based upon the object of search,
weather in the search area, electronic or visual location aids used, and any other known factor,
which would affect the search altitude. Generally, the lower the search altitude, the better the
chance of seeing an object, provided the altitude is not so low that the aircraft speed becomes
detrimental. During preliminary searches, the altitude will be higher to detect possible signals at
greater distances. Also, see Table 24.7
24.3. Search Procedures.
   24.3.1. Rescue missions often involve commitment based upon calculated risks. Give full
   consideration to all safety factors. Timely reaction to all search missions is essential to the
   safety of personnel or equipment. Do not jeopardize safety by inadequate preparation or
   short cuts to expedite takeoff, or arrival at search areas. Aircrews will comply with all other
   applicable directives, which govern their duties, such as:
       24.3.1.1. Accomplish complete predeparture flight planning except for scramble
       missions. On scramble missions, complete essential flight planning prior to and shortly
       after takeoff.
       24.3.1.2. Use in-flight procedures in accordance with the appropriate theater of operation
       flight regulations.
           24.3.1.2.1. Aircraft will maintain vertical and horizontal separation in the search
           area. This is critical when several aircraft are searching within close proximity.

Table 24.7. Recommended Search Altitudes.

             24.3.1.2.2. Transmit operation normal (position) reports as required by the
             controlling agency.
   24.3.2. The following general instructions apply to all search missions:
          24.3.2.1. Brief crew members that did not attend the operations briefing on the purpose
          of the mission.
          24.3.2.2. Scanners who are not aircrew members will receive a briefing on the search
          objective.
          24.3.2.3. The PIC will supervise and coordinate activities of crew members during
          preparation for search, as follows:
             24.3.2.3.1. Plan the search with the navigator(s).
             24.3.2.3.2. Discuss with all crew members scanning procedures.
             24.3.2.3.3. Discuss with the navigator(s) and copilot the procedures for making
             search pattern turns.
             24.3.2.3.4. Discuss the radio communication procedures with the AMSS and copilot.
             24.3.2.3.5. Discuss the preparation of flares, sea dyes, and smoke signals for
             deployment with the loadmaster.
             24.3.2.3.6. Discuss the preparation of personnel and equipment for airborne delivery
             with the loadmaster and pararescue personnel. Ensure all crew members are
             thoroughly familiar with the checklists to be used for various airborne delivery
             options.
          24.3.2.4. The PIC will coordinate crew member activities during prosecution of the
          search by:
             24.3.2.4.1. Ensuring completion of the SEARCH checklist prior to commencing any
             low-level search.
202                                                AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011


         24.3.2.4.2. Alerting the scanners to begin scanning when approaching the search
         area.
         24.3.2.4.3. Directing crew members to put on their LPUs prior to descending below
         2,000 ft over water.
         24.3.2.4.4. Checking with crew members to ensure all equipment and personnel are
         ready for action in the event of a sighting.
         24.3.2.4.5. Making periodic checks of fuel remaining to ensure sufficient fuel
         remains for return to home station.
      24.3.2.5. Report all deviations from assigned search procedures to the on-scene
      commander or mission commander.
      24.3.2.6. Thoroughly investigate sightings and report findings immediately. Initiate
      recovery action or assistance when the survivors are located and inform appropriate
      agencies of the progress.
      24.3.2.7. The navigator(s) will:
         24.3.2.7.1. Maintain an accurate record of area searched.
         24.3.2.7.2. Direct the aircraft to ensure proper coverage of the search area.
         24.3.2.7.3. Continually cross-check headings, drift, time, airspeed, and altitude to
         maintain the best possible search coverage.
         24.3.2.7.4. Use a large-scale chart when searching over land to ensure terrain and
         obstacle clearance. Use a search pattern graph as a suitable reference while searching
         over water.
         24.3.2.7.5. Record sighting information on log and plot position on navigation chart.
         24.3.2.7.6. Monitor equipment during radar and electronic searches.
         24.3.2.7.7. Debrief controlling agency with an accurate depiction of any areas
         searched.
         24.3.2.7.8. When other aircraft are involved, with the assistance of the AMSS, track
         and assign search areas for other aircraft.
      24.3.2.8. The AMSS will maintain radio communications with other search aircraft and
      the controlling agency as directed by the PIC.
      24.3.2.9. The loadmaster will assume supervision of the scanners and will schedule
      rotation and rest periods. Scanners should be rotated every 30 to 45 minutes of scanning
      and rested after two hours, if enough scanners are available.
      24.3.2.10. All crew members will assist with the scanning duties when possible.
      24.3.2.11. When a search is completed with negative results, consider searching the area
      again. Normally, position subsequent search legs between or 45° to the previous search
      legs. This procedure, in effect, results in smaller track spacing.
  24.3.3. In the search area:
      24.3.3.1. Descend to search altitude (Table 24.7).
AFI11-2MC-130V3 22 DECEMBER 2011                                                                        203


          24.3.3.2. Have navigator obtain the wind at search altitude. To visually cover the area
          thoroughly, slow the airspeed during search patterns (refer to the Aircraft Performance
          Manual).
          24.3.3.3. Notify ATC of arrival on scene and estimated endurance.
          24.3.3.4. Vector other aircraft to the scene.
          24.3.3.5. Use radar (some life rafts carry reflectors).
          24.3.3.6. Assume on-scene command until relieved by another duly appointed SAR
          aircraft. As other aircraft arrive, do the following:
              24.3.3.6.1. Establish contact on channels other than Guard.
              24.3.3.6.2. Obtain aircraft type identification, endurance, and rescue capability.
              24.3.3.6.3. Assign altimeter setting, frequencies, search areas, patterns, and altitude
              separation.
          24.3.3.7. If leaving the search area because of lack of fuel, assign another aircraft as on-
          scene commander.
      24.3.4. Determine an accurate wind prior to and throughout the search by SCNS, INS, or
      Doppler. Use Table 24.9 to estimate wind velocity near the surface.
      24.3.5. When flying search patterns, turns must be accurate and uniform as possible. The
      following procedures are provided to assist the aircrew in making precise turns:
          24.3.5.1. Fly the aircraft on autopilot when possible.
          24.3.5.2. Prior to starting the pattern, the navigator will brief the crew on the direction
          and rate of turn and the turn command procedure used.
          24.3.5.3. Normally, all turns are standard rate (3° per second) unless track spacing is less
          in distance than the diameter of a standard turn at a given speed. In this situation,
          continue turn to 180° and adjust turn rate as required to remain within the pattern.
